1. Create a new DSPy Signature `ExtractKeyEntities` that takes `claim` and `passages` as input and outputs `entities` (a list of specific named entities, dates, locations, organizations, and person names from the documents).

2. Modify the query generation for hops 2 and 3 to accept BOTH the existing summary AND a new `entities` field containing the accumulated entities from previous hops.

3. Update the flow to maintain two parallel tracks:
   - **Abstract Track**: Keep existing summarization logic (summary_1, summary_2)
   - **Entity Track**: After each hop, extract entities from retrieved documents and accumulate them (entities_1, entities_1+entities_2)

4. For hop 2, pass both `summary_1` and `entities_1` to query generation.
5. For hop 3, pass `summary_1`, `summary_2`, `entities_1`, and `entities_2` to query generation.

6. Update signature strings to: `"claim,summary_1,entities_1->query"` for hop 2 and `"claim,summary_1,summary_2,entities_1,entities_2->query"` for hop 3.

This dual-track approach prevents the loss of specific entity names during summarization while maintaining semantic context, enabling more precise retrieval in later hops. The entity extraction module should focus on preserving exact names, dates, and specific identifiers that are critical for finding the right supporting documents.

Iteration 19: Proposed new text for extract_entities: Task Description:

You are given a single claim (a sentence or a short paragraph). Your goal is to identify and extract 3 to 5 named entities from the claim that are most relevant as supporting facts to verify or refute the claim. These entities must be people, places, organizations, or specific works (such as films, books, artworks, or events) that are closely tied to the factual content of the claim and likely to be found in a knowledge base or fact-checking documents. 

Detailed Guidelines:

1. Entity Types: Extract named entities that fall into these categories:
   - People (e.g., authors, directors, actors, historical figures, scientists)
   - Places (e.g., cities, regions, geographic landmarks, institutions tied to places)
   - Organizations (e.g., companies, universities, creative groups)
   - Specific works (e.g., books, films, TV shows, musical albums, artworks) including specific editions or versions when indicated.

2. Focus on Supporting Facts:
   - Extract entities that are directly referenced in or are essential to understanding the factual basis of the claim.
   - Prefer entities that are known to be cited in supporting evidence or knowledge bases (e.g., authoritative names of authors, exact title of works, correct versions of shows or films).
   - Generic or vague terms (e.g., "the 1900 establishment", "the namesake individual") are not sufficient unless they correspond to a recognized named entity in the claim context.
   - Dates or time periods (e.g., "1960s") should only be included if they function as a named entity tied to a fact (e.g., specific historical periods or events). Otherwise, treat them as contextual information.

3. Correctness and Disambiguation:
   - Use the full and accurate names of people or works when possible (e.g., "Ali Qushji" not "Ali Qushiji"; "The Secret Agent (TV series)", not just "The Secret Agent").
   - Include precise titles of works including disambiguators (e.g., year or medium) when they clarify the reference.
   - Include relevant organizations precisely (e.g., "Swinburne University of Technology" rather than vague campus names).
   - When a claim involves relationships or roles (e.g., "director of Mulholland Falls"), identify both the person (director) and the work (film) by their recognized entity names.

4. Number of Entities:
   - Extract at least three but no more than five entities.
   - If fewer than three entities are factual or relevant, extract only those necessary.
   - Avoid including unrelated entities, or entities not supported by the claim’s semantics.

5. Strategy for Extraction:
   - Identify all named entities explicitly mentioned in the claim.
   - Identify implicit or inferred named entities when the claim strongly depends on them (e.g., the author of a specific book referenced by title).
   - Ensure to capture entities that appear multiple times or whose relationship to the claim can be cross-validated.
   - Avoid generic descriptions or placeholders; use specific, recognized entities.

6. Formatting:
   - Provide the extracted entities as a list of names or titles.
   - Each entity should be properly capitalized and formatted as a proper noun.
   - Do not include numeric labels or roles—only pure named entities.

Example:

Claim: "Both the director of Mulholland Falls and Robert Redford work as a director."
Entities: Mulholland Falls, Lee Tamahori, Robert Redford

Explanation: Here, “Mulholland Falls” (film), “Lee Tamahori” (director of that film), and “Robert Redford” (person referenced by name) are the key supporting facts.

---

This task requires domain-specific knowledge of historical figures, creative works, organizations, and their relationships. You should leverage this knowledge to select the most relevant supporting facts named entities referenced or implied in the claim that would support fact verification.

Iteration 21: Proposed new text for entity_to_query: You will be given an entity name and a claim context that references factual assertions involving that entity and related entities, events, or concepts. Your task is to convert this information into a concise, detailed, and retrieval-optimized search query that would effectively retrieve evidence supporting or refuting the claim in a knowledge base or search engine.

To do this:

1. Identify the key entities, dates, events, or specific relationships mentioned in the claim context related to the given entity.

2. Include relevant aliases, variations, or closely related proper nouns for the entity and related elements that help target the correct knowledge sources (e.g., alternative names, specific years, associated people or organizations).

3. Extract and incorporate critical domain-specific details implied or explicitly stated in the claim, such as:
   - Time frames (years, seasons, dates)
   - Roles or relationships (e.g., “head coach,” “producer,” “member,” “author”)
   - Specific titles of works (films, albums, books, shows) and their metadata (release year, awards, nominations)
   - Geographic or organizational contexts (locations, companies, sports teams)
   - Connections between entities (e.g., collaborations, predecessors, successors)

4. Use search query operators and syntax where relevant:
   - Quotation marks for exact phrases or titles
   - OR for synonyms or related terms
   - AND or implied conjunction for combining distinct concepts
   - Parentheses if multiple alternatives are included together

5. Formulate the query so it reflects the logical structure of the claim: include comparative facts (like birth order or operational dates), negations, or overlapping information (such as “not,” “before,” “also known as”).

6. Avoid adding speculative or unrelated terms; focus only on elements directly connected to verifying the claim.

7. When appropriate, include relevant known related entities from the claim context to expand the search’s reach while maintaining focus.

8. The overall query should be succinct but comprehensive enough to return evidence-rich results that directly pertain to the claim, facilitating verifiability.

Example generalizable strategy:  
- Parse the claim context to extract specific facts, dates, and relationships.  
- Map these to key named entities, terms, and factual anchors.  
- Combine these with the entity name to build a targeted search query incorporating known domain-specific vocabulary and search operators for precision.

Remember, the goal is not merely to restate the entity but to craft a factually rich, well-structured query that optimizes retrieval of precise supporting or refuting information for the given claim context.

Extract entities and relationships from retrieved docs using EntityExtractor.\n   - **Iteration 2**: Use GapAnalysis to identify missing entities/relationships. Generate targeted queries for each missing entity. Retrieve k=5 docs per missing entity (up to 3 entities = 15 docs max). Update entity/relationship store.\n   - **Iteration 3**: Final gap analysis. Generate specific queries for any remaining missing pieces. Retrieve k=5 docs per query (up to 3 queries = 15 docs max).\n   - **After iterations**: Pool all retrieved documents (~40 total), deduplicate, score with DocumentRelevanceScorer, return top 21.\n\n3. **Key architectural benefits**:\n   - Explicitly discovers implicit entities through decomposition (solves Root Cause #1)\n   - Each iteration builds structured knowledge (entities + relationships), not just summaries\n   - GapAnalysis provides self-correction by identifying what's missing before next retrieval\n   - Sub-questions break complex claims into retrievable units\n   - Still respects 3-search constraint (3 iterations, each potentially doing 1 batch retrieval operation)\n\nImplementation location: /workspace/langProBe/hover/hover_pipeline.py, within the HoverMultiHopPipeline class. [AGENT] Change request (full): Implement a "Query Decomposition with Iterative Entity Discovery" architecture in HoverMultiHopPipeline.forward() that transforms the multi-hop retrieval from similarity search to structured reasoning:

**Core Change**: Replace the current linear 3-hop retrieval with an iterative entity-driven approach:

1. **Create new DSPy Signature classes** (in hover_pipeline.py):
   - `ClaimDecomposition`: Takes claim → outputs list of sub-questions (e.g., "Who directed The River Rat?", "What else did that person create?")
   - `EntityExtractor`: Takes claim + retrieved documents → outputs structured list of key entities and relationships discovered
   - `GapAnalysis`: Takes claim + entities_found + documents_retrieved → outputs what critical information is still missing

2. **Modify HoverMultiHopPipeline.forward()** to implement iterative discovery loop (max 3 iterations to satisfy constraint):
   - **Iteration 1**: Decompose claim into 2-3 sub-questions using ClaimDecomposition signature. For each sub-question, retrieve k=5 documents (total 10-15 docs). Extract entities and relationships from retrieved docs using EntityExtractor.
   - **Iteration 2**: Use GapAnalysis to identify missing entities/relationships. Generate targeted queries for each missing entity. Retrieve k=5 docs per missing entity (up to 3 entities = 15 docs max). Update entity/relationship store.
   - **Iteration 3**: Final gap analysis. Generate specific queries for any remaining missing pieces. Retrieve k=5 docs per query (up to 3 queries = 15 docs max).
   - **After iterations**: Pool all retrieved documents (~40 total), deduplicate, score with DocumentRelevanceScorer, return top 21.

3. **Key architectural benefits**:
   - Explicitly discovers implicit entities through decomposition (solves Root Cause #1)
   - Each iteration builds structured knowledge (entities + relationships), not just summaries
   - GapAnalysis provides self-correction by identifying what's missing before next retrieval
   - Sub-questions break complex claims into retrievable units
   - Still respects 3-search constraint (3 iterations, each potentially doing 1 batch retrieval operation)

Implementation location: /workspace/langProBe/hover/hover_pipeline.py, within the HoverMultiHopPipeline class. Remove or deprecate the HoverMultiHop module dependency as the new architecture handles iteration directly in the parent forward() method.

Iteration 23: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 24: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 25: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Task Description: 

You are given a claim and a set of retrieved documents relevant to verifying the claim. Your goal is to extract the key concrete entities and relationships from these documents that are pertinent to supporting or refuting the claim.

Input Format:  
- A "claim" statement—a factual assertion potentially involving entities such as people, organizations, places, dates, events, artworks, media productions, or other concrete subjects.  
- A set of paragraph-level or item-level "documents" that provide information, background, or context relevant to the claim.  

Key Objectives: 

1. Identify and extract **concrete entities** from the documents that are relevant to the claim. Focus especially on:  
   - People (with birth, death dates if available) who may be actors, authors, political figures, artists, or other notable roles.  
   - Places such as cities, states, countries, venues, or institutions.  
   - Organizations (e.g., political parties, sports teams, universities, companies).  
   - Dates and events (years, specific dates, or named events relevant to the claim).  
   - Works of art, films, TV shows, novels, albums, songs, or similar cultural artifacts.  
   - Roles (characters, professional roles, titles) connected to these entities.  

2. Extract **relationships** that directly connect these entities and that are relevant to the claim verification. These relationships should be factual propositions or statements documented in the texts, for example:
   - "X is the author of Y."  
   - "X was born on [date]."  
   - "X played role R in film F."  
   - "X is the current holder of position P in organization O."  
   - "Organization A is based in location L."  
   - "Event E occurred on date D."  
   - "Film F was released in year Y."  
   - "X and Y co-starred in film F."  
   - "Book B was first published in year Y."  
   - "Entity X is part of organization Y."  

3. Ensure to capture **disambiguating details** about entities and relationships that may be critical to differentiate similar or easily confused subjects. For example:  
   - Distinguishing between individuals with similar names by birthdate or place.  
   - Distinguishing different works with similar or identical names by author, director, or year.  
   - Differentiating date ranges or versions of institutions when applicable (e.g., different universities or locations).  

4. When a claim involves negation or contradiction (e.g., "X was not …", "Y does not…"), identify relevant relationships or lack thereof that support or refute these negative claims.

5. If parts of the claim cannot be directly confirmed or disproven from the retrieved documents, clearly indicate that no supporting relationship or fact is extracted for those parts.

6. Your extracted entities and relationships should be:
   - Concrete and specifically relevant to the claim.
   - Factually grounded in the provided documents only.
   - Free from speculation or inference beyond the provided information.
   - Explicitly linked to the claim verification task (focus the extraction on what is pertinent).

Generalizable Strategy: 

- Parse the claim and identify core entities and concepts requiring evidence (people, works, dates, places, roles).  
- Systematically scan each document to identify mention of these core entities or any related ones.  
- Extract and normalize entities with distinguishing attributes (dates, professions, birthplaces, versions) to avoid ambiguity.  
- Extract explicit factual propositions about these entities, especially connections aligning with claim components.  
- Note conflict or inconsistency within documents and between documents and claim.  
- Include relevant contextual entities and their relationships when supporting claim elements (e.g., organization affiliations, chronological order, title holdings).  

By following this systematic, entity-centric and claim-focused extraction approach, you provide a clear and accurate foundation for verifying or refuting complex factual claims spanning multiple domains including politics, arts, sports, academia, entertainment, and history.

Iteration 26: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 27: Proposed new text for extract_entities: You will be given a claim, which is a short text containing one or more factual statements. Your task is to analyze this claim to identify and extract between 3 to 5 named entities that serve as likely supporting facts or key pieces of evidence referenced by the claim. These named entities should be specific and relevant to verifying or supporting the claim and can include:

- People (e.g., individuals named or clearly implied in the claim)
- Places (cities, states, countries, or specific locations)
- Organizations (companies, institutions, political bodies)
- Specific works (films, albums, books, TV series, artworks)
- Specific events, awards, or dates, if explicitly named

Key Details and Guidance:

1. **Select entities that directly support or verify parts of the claim.** The entities you choose should be those that would be referenced as factual evidence when fact-checking or verifying the claim, not just any entity mentioned.

2. **Be as specific as possible.** For example, if the claim references a film, prefer the full, formal title of the film (e.g., "Batman: Mask of the Phantasm" or "Purple Rain (album)") rather than vague or partial references. Similarly, if a person is mentioned, extract the full recognized name.

3. **Disambiguation matters.** Where multiple similarly named entities exist (e.g., multiple districts or similarly titled works), choose the one most specifically matching the claim context.

4. **Include relevant proper nouns regardless of explicit label in the claim.** For example, if the claim refers to "the director of Batman: Mask of the Phantasm," include the director's name as an entity even if not explicitly named, if you can infer it from the claim or context.

5. **Avoid non-specific references.** Do not include vague phrases like "the company," "the director," or "the show's host" as entities unless they are replaced or identified by a proper noun.

6. **Entities related by association or supporting detail are important.** For instance, if a claim references a prequel or sequel, or the original work inspiring another, include both works as entities if they are core to the claim.

7. **Dates and numbers:** Only include dates or numbers as entities if they are explicitly named as significant facts or titles (e.g., “May 26, 2013” in relation to a show’s premiere).

8. **Limit to 3-5 entities.** Excessive or irrelevant entities dilute the focus. Choose the core relevant named entities that are necessary for supporting or verifying the claim.

9. **If multiple potential entities are relevant, prioritize those that would be core evidence or well-known reference points for fact-checking the claim.**

10. **Format:** Respond with a list of entities, each as a proper noun string exactly as known/referenced in the claim or supporting data.

The goal is to select named entities that, when linked to their corresponding knowledge (such as Wikipedia pages), would provide the factual support or context necessary to verify the claim’s truthfulness or details.

Example: For the claim "The director of Batman: Mask of the Phantasm produced Avengers Assemble," the proper entities to extract are “Eric Radomski,” “Batman: Mask of the Phantasm,” and “Avengers Assemble,” because these are the distinct named facts that verify and support the claim.

Your output should only be the list of these entities (people, places, organizations, works) with no additional explanation or formatting.

Iteration 28: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 29: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Task Description:
You are given a claim and a set of retrieved documents related to the claim. Your goal is to extract and present key concrete entities and relationships from these documents that are relevant to verifying the claim. The focus is on identifying factual and specific information about named entities, such as people, places, organizations, dates, events, works of art (films, operas, songs, plays), awards, and other domain-specific objects, along with how these entities relate to each other.

Detailed Instructions:
1. **Entities Extraction:**
   - Extract concrete entities including but not limited to:
     - People (including historical figures, artists, directors, actors, musicians, scholars)
     - Places (countries, cities, regions, venues, landmarks)
     - Organizations (companies, studios, university systems, festivals, institutions)
     - Dates and time ranges (years, specific dates of events or releases)
     - Events (award ceremonies, festivals, tournaments, film premieres, releases)
     - Artistic works (films, plays, operas, songs, albums, music videos, board games)
     - Roles and titles related to entities (e.g., "director", "producer", "character played")
     - Other relevant domain terms (e.g., breeds of dogs, grape varieties, product brands)

2. **Relationship Extraction:**
   - Extract relationships that directly connect entities in ways relevant to verifying the claim. Examples include:
     - Who directed, produced, acted in, or wrote a particular film, play, or song
     - Where and when a film or event was released, premiered, or held
     - Associations such as "person X is from place Y", "organization Z is located in place Y"
     - Historical or familial connections (e.g., co-emperors, successors)
     - Product or brand ownership, specialties, or functions (e.g., manufacturer of a device, specialty of a company)
     - Award nominations and recipients, and for which works
     - Specific roles within films (e.g., characters played by actors)
     - Relationships between artistic works and their influences, inspirations, or theme references
     - Locations associated with festivals or events

3. **Comprehensive Reasoning:**
   - Use the extracted entities and relationships to form a detailed reasoning explanation about the claim's veracity.
   - Clarify whether the information in the documents supports, contradicts, or insufficiently addresses the claim.
   - Mention any ambiguities or missing information relevant to the claim verification.

4. **Presentation Format:**
   - Provide a "reasoning" section summarizing your logical analysis of the claim based on the documents.
   - List extracted entities clearly, categorized or labeled by type for clarity (e.g., 'Person:', 'Film:', 'Place:', 'Date:', 'Organization:').
   - List key relationships expressed in simple, factual sentences linking entities.
   - Include relevant dates or timeframes as part of entities or relationships to show temporal context.

5. **Domain-Specific Nuances:**
   - Be aware that returned documents may include information with overlapping or ambiguous terms (e.g., multiple works with similar titles, different individuals with similar names).
   - Understand that verifying a claim often involves cross-linking multiple entities and facts: for example, linking an actor to a film and a character, the year of the film's release, the director, and any relevant awards.
   - When a claim involves comparative or negation constructs (e.g., "not this but that"), carefully extract and contrast facts supporting each part.
   - Recognize special relationships like artistic inspiration, thematic references, or naming origins (e.g., a dance named after a people).
   - Accept that some information may not be explicitly present in the documents; identify such gaps and note inability to fully verify from the data.

6. **Handling Ambiguities and Partial Evidence:**
   - Explicitly state when a document lacks sufficient evidence to confirm part or all of the claim.
   - If multiple versions or interpretations exist (e.g., different films with the same name), clarify which one is relevant based on provided dates or directors.
   - If the claim involves temporal assertions, ensure the correct chronological order is established based on document data.

7. **Avoiding Assumptions Beyond Documents:**
   - Do not rely on external or common-knowledge data not contained in provided documents.
   - Report only factually supported entities and relationships.
   - When external knowledge conflicts with document content, prioritize the documents.

Summary:
Extract precise, concrete entities and their factual, verifiable relationships from the documents relevant to the given claim. Use these extractions to reason about the claim's validity, noting any evidential gaps or contradictions. Format your output consistently into "reasoning," "entities," and "relationships" sections, labeling entities by type and expressing relationships clearly and simply.

Iteration 31: Proposed new text for gap_analyzer.predict: You are given a claim along with structured intermediate findings from document retrieval and entity-relationship extraction steps. Your task is to analyze what critical information remains missing that prevents conclusive verification of the claim, given the entities, relationships, and documents retrieved so far.

Specifically, you must:

1. Identify explicitly the precise missing factual information, entities, relationships, or authoritative evidence required to fully verify or refute the claim. This includes:
   - Missing key attributes (e.g., birthdates, dates, memberships)
   - Missing authoritative source confirmations (e.g., primary credits, official biographical data, liner notes, company reports)
   - Disambiguations needed (e.g., confirming which entity exactly is referenced when names or terms are ambiguous)
   - Clarifications or definitions of ambiguous terms or roles (e.g., "filmmaker behind" meaning director)
   - Relevant temporal context to compare entities appropriately (e.g., membership counts at a given time, or dates of involvement)
   - Verification of whether conflicting or contradictory assertions in different document sets need resolution
   - Confirmation of any potentially conflated or miscited identities or organizations in the claims (e.g., confusion between persons and organizations with similar names)

2. Formulate missing information as specific, detailed information requests or factual points that, if acquired, enable a definitive claim verification.

3. Produce targeted queries or search prompts that would most effectively yield the missing information in the next retrieval iteration. These should be concrete, contain key named entities, dates, and context-sensitive keywords, and suggest authoritative domains (e.g., official websites, academic resources, reputable databases like IMDb, Discogs, company filings, or bibliographic sources).

4. Provide a concise, logical reasoning summary that:
   - Synthesizes what has been established so far relevant to the claim
   - Explains why the current evidence is insufficient
   - Identifies what precise gaps prevent final verification
   - States why the identified missing information is critical

Notes on the task and domain-specific considerations:

- Claims often pertain to biographical data, film credits, discographies, company ownership stakes, event results, or publication histories. Handling specificity and disambiguation is crucial.

- Domains span historical timelines, entertainment industry (films, music albums, awards, directors), publishing (magazines, readership demographics), taxonomy (botanical classification), aviation ownership, and others.

- Detailed entity resolution is necessary: persons with similar or variant names, bands with overlapping names, films with alternate release years, or organizations with similar titles.

- Temporal context matters: verifying whether an attribute applies at a certain point in history, or comparing contemporaneous entities (e.g., band membership during a specific album).

- The assistant’s output should systematically highlight missing critical information for claim verification, supporting logical next-step retrieval queries.

- The reasoning and missing information outputs should be supported implicitly by the discovered entities and relationships, pointing out contradictions or incomplete evidence to the user.

- Use domain-appropriate terminology, and tailor targeted queries to typical authoritative sources known in the domain (e.g., IMDb for films or music databases for discographies).

Your output must include:

- A reasoning paragraph explaining the verification gaps and necessity for further information.

- A bullet-point list of detailed missing information or evidence required.

- A bullet-point list of explicit, well-formed targeted queries optimized for the next iteration of information retrieval.

This process serves to guide iterative evidence gathering for claim verification by highlighting critical gaps and optimizing further search strategies.

[TIMER] propose_new_texts took 21.53s
Iteration 32: Proposed new text for entity_title_extractor.predict: Task: Extract potential Wikipedia article titles (proper nouns) from the given claim and its retrieved context. 

Purpose: Identify the specific named entities (people, places, events, organizations, artistic works such as books, films, albums, etc.) referenced or implied in the claim and context that correspond to actual or plausible Wikipedia article titles. These entities should be those that would typically have standalone Wikipedia pages.

Input format:
- "claim": A statement with one or more references to people, places, organizations, events, or works.
- "context": Supporting or related information that may mention additional entities or help clarify ambiguous references in the claim.

Output format:
- A list of entity titles exactly as they would appear as Wikipedia article titles.

Key Guidelines:

1. **Entity types to extract:**
   - People (full names, or disambiguated forms if necessary, e.g., 'Dave Evans (singer)')
   - Places (towns, neighborhoods, countries, roads, landmarks)
   - Events (specific named historical or sports events, awards, tournaments)
   - Organizations (clubs, teams, companies, institutes, leagues)
   - Works of art and media (films, albums, books, TV shows, plays) in their correct Wikipedia title form
   - Other named entities that would have their own Wikipedia articles

2. **Exact Wikipedia article title form:**
   - Use the exact canonical Wikipedia article title for the entity as found or expected.
   - Include disambiguation terms if present in the context to avoid ambiguity ('Deep Purple (album)', 'Dave Evans (singer)').
   - For works with multiple versions or adaptations (films, TV series, albums), extract the version or disambiguation with year or format as provided or implied.
   - Do not generalize or paraphrase. Use the precise names as given.

3. **Relevance & Direct relation:**
   - Extract entities directly relevant to verifying the claim or mentioned explicitly or implicitly in the context supporting the claim.
   - Extract entities that are necessary for understanding or verifying factual statements in the claim.
   - Do not include overly broad or generic concepts that would not have individual Wikipedia pages.

4. **Handling missing explicit names:**
   - If the claim references a role (e.g., "host of The Colbert Report") and the context names the person fulfilling that role, include the person's Wikipedia title as the key entity.
   - If the claim refers to an entity by a description only (e.g., "the 1986 American science-fiction comedy directed by Willard Huyck"), include the known or strongly implied entity (e.g., 'Howard the Duck (film)').
   - Avoid inventing or guessing entities not supported by context or external well-known equivalences unless clearly implied.

5. **Context utilization:**
   - Use the context to confirm and disambiguate entity titles.
   - Extract additional proper nouns from the context if they help clarify or verify the claim.
   - Include sponsors or alternative names for entities/events if they serve as formal Wikipedia article titles or are critical to identification.

6. **Do not include:**
   - Common nouns or general descriptive terms without a unique Wikipedia article (e.g., 'football club' without a specific proper noun attached).
   - Entities only named in passing without relevance or explicit mention.
   - Ambiguous or overly generic entries unless disambiguated by context.

7. **Formatting:**
   - Provide the output as a JSON-like list of strings of Wikipedia article titles.
   - No additional explanation or formatting is needed beyond the list of strings.

Example Workflow:
- Identify named entities in claim.
- Use context to find exact Wikipedia article titles, including disambiguation where required.
- Add relevant entities from context that support or clarify claim entities.
- Ensure correctness and completeness based on provided data.

Remember this process focuses on precision, specificity, and relevance to the factual claim supported by the context.

Iteration 33: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 34: Proposed new text for scorer.scorer.predict: You will be given a **claim** and a **document**. Your task is to evaluate how relevant the document is to verifying the truthfulness or accuracy of the claim. Assign a relevance score from **1 to 10** where:

- **10** means the document is highly relevant and provides critical factual evidence that directly supports or refutes the claim, including details sufficient to confirm names, dates, specific relationships, or events central to the claim.
- **1** means the document is completely irrelevant to the claim and does not provide any useful information for verification.

### Important details and domain-specific considerations:
- The claim and document often mention **proper nouns** such as people (e.g., actors, musicians, politicians), works (e.g., films, operas, songs), locations (e.g., airports, parks), organizations (e.g., football clubs, political parties), or specific events (e.g., sports tournaments). Pay attention to matching these key entities.
- The document is relevant only if it contains **direct or strongly implied evidence** that supports or refutes some aspect of the claim, such as:
  - The **number of acts** or characteristics of an opera or film.
  - **Biographical details** including birthdates, occupations, collaborations, or awards of named individuals.
  - **Event outcomes** such as tournament winners or release dates of movies.
  - **Associations or relationships** between people (e.g., friendships, co-starring, directorial connections).
  - **Geographic proximity or affiliations** related to locations mentioned in the claim.
  - **Sponsorship or membership information** related to organizations or teams.
- The document may **mention related or similar topics but must provide factual information clearly connected to the claim’s key elements** in order to justify a score higher than 1.
- If the document only shares **tangential or vague context** without directly addressing any claim component, assign a low score (usually between 1 and 3).
- Carefully evaluate if the document **explicitly confirms, contradicts, or provides key missing details** necessary for claim verification.
- Use the document’s content to reason step-by-step whether or not it supports or is relevant to the claim before assigning the score.
- Consider **typographical errors** or slight name variations in the claim when they might correspond to entities in the document.
- In case of ambiguity or partial relevance, assign a medium score (e.g., 4-7) reflecting partial but not full support.

### Output format:
- Provide a **brief reasoning paragraph** explaining your assessment of relevance based on the document content and the claim.
- Provide the **final relevance score** as an integer from 1 to 10.

This task requires careful reading of both claim and document, precise extraction of domain-specific knowledge (e.g., opera acts, political roles, filmographies), and discerning direct relevance from mere associative or background information. Always prioritize concrete evidence directly relating to the entities and relationships cited in the claim.

Iteration 35: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:
You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a claim containing multiple linked pieces of factual information that must be verified collectively to determine the claim’s truthfulness. Your task is to decompose this complex claim into 2-3 specific, clearly delineated sub-questions. Each sub-question should be independently answerable and should target a distinct key piece of information necessary to verify the claim. The goal is to break down the claim logically into smaller verification steps that correspond to the individual facts or entities mentioned or implied in the claim.

Key Details and Domain-Specific Considerations:
1. **Distinct Information Pieces:**  
   Each sub-question must target a unique information element that represents one fact or concept from the claim. This includes people’s identities, their works, dates (birth, release, death), relationships (collaborations, marital status), roles (director, actor, composer), geographical or organizational data (locations, institutions), and other domain-specific entities (films, songs, artworks, dog breeds, religious figures or beliefs, awards).

2. **Disambiguation and Specificity:**  
   - If the claim mentions ambiguous references (e.g., a person with multiple films, multiple possible film versions, or unspecific titles), the sub-questions should include clarifying or identifying the correct referent before seeking the fact (e.g., "Which film directed by X is referenced?" or "Which version of the film Y?").  
   - Include context in sub-questions to specify which entity or timeframe is intended (e.g., band lineup at the time of album release, primary filming locations, or award nomination year).  

3. **Multi-hop Logical Flow:**  
   The decomposition should reflect the logical steps required to verify the claim:  
   - Identify entities (people, works, places, organizations).  
   - Establish attributes or properties of those entities (roles, dates, nationality, collaborations).  
   - Draw comparisons or relationships that the claim asserts (e.g., comparing dates, membership size, professional scopes, production credits).  

4. **Addressing Potential Ambiguities in Claims:**  
   When claims combine facts from different sources, times, or entities, sub-questions must separate these facts clearly and, if necessary, confirm the linking or equivalence intended by the claim (e.g., verifying if two naming references refer to the same person/place, or if a named role or nomination corresponds to the same actor).

5. **Fact Types Commonly Targeted:**  
   - Personal biographical data: birthdates, nationalities, death dates, marital status.  
   - Professional credits: director, producer, actor roles, authorship, collaboration frequency.  
   - Work specifics: film/song titles, release dates, genres, filming locations.  
   - Organizational data: award ceremony data, institutional enrollment or size, membership counts.  
   - Geographic and naming origins: what places are named after whom, adjacent landmarks, country affiliations.  
   - Religious/mythological elements: deity attributes, religious practices, beliefs on afterlife or judgment.

6. **Comparative or Quantitative Claims:**  
   For claims asserting comparisons (e.g., “more members than,” “before,” “more scope of profession”), sub-questions should separately retrieve the relevant quantities or attributes for each entity involved and (if needed) frame a question concerning the comparison basis, so verification can proceed stepwise.

7. **Use Neutral, Unbiased Wording:**  
   The sub-questions should be framed objectively, without presuming the answer or the claim’s truth, phrased as information-seeking queries.

Instruction for Assistant:

- Carefully parse the entire claim and identify discrete factual nodes essential to verify it.
- For each node, write a precise, factual question that can be answered independently.  
- Aim for 2-3 sub-questions per claim, covering all necessary elements to verify the claim’s truth.  
- When ambiguity or multiple interpretations are possible, include a clarifying sub-question to disambiguate first.  
- If the claim involves relationships or comparisons among entities, separate questions to collect comparable data from each entity before enabling the final verification step.  
- Ensure your sub-questions reflect domain-specific nuances such as film release versions, lineup timeframes, and award details as appropriate for the claim context.  
- Each sub-question must be focused, specific, and answerable with reliable factual sources.

By following these principles, your output will enable systematic fact-checking of complex multi-hop claims across a wide range of domains such as film, music, biography, geography, religion, and more.

Iteration 37: Proposed new text for gap_analyzer.predict: Your task is to assist in the verification of factual claims by analyzing the given partial evidence—entities, relationships, and documents retrieved so far—and determining what critical information remains missing to conclusively verify the claim. 

Specifically, for each claim, you will be provided with:  
- A **claim** (a factual statement to verify)  
- A list of **entities found** (persons, organizations, places, works, dates, and concepts relevant to the claim)  
- A set of **relationships found** linking these entities (e.g., person X directed film Y, dates of events, authorship, roles, birth dates)  
- The **number of documents retrieved** so far (indicating evidence collected)  

Based on these inputs:  
1. **Analyze which parts of the claim have been sufficiently addressed or supported by the retrieved entities and relationships.**  
   - Identify which key facts, links, or data points have been discovered that align with the claim.  
   - Summarize the reasoning on how the existing data supports or fails to support specific claim components.  

2. **Identify *critical missing information* necessary to fully verify the claim**—including:  
   - Missing explicit dates (e.g., birthdates, founding years, release dates) of people, organizations, or works to establish temporal order.  
   - Missing primary or authoritative evidence confirming roles, authorship, participation, or connections (e.g., official cast lists, program rosters, film credits, historical records).  
   - Missing confirmation on geographical or organizational relationships (e.g., whether a magazine’s editorial office is located in a certain locale).  
   - Any contradictions or ambiguities that need resolving (e.g., conflicting dates, unclear identity of referenced entities, or possible conflation of works or persons).  
   - Clarifications regarding ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (e.g., “the star” of a work, or the intended work if multiple versions exist).  
   - Need for official or primary documents (e.g., university records, naval action reports, authoritative bibliographies, reputable databases such as IMDb, AFI, TCM, or institutional websites).  
   - Biographical or filmographical details that provide necessary context for verification (e.g., career timelines, residence histories).  

3. **Formulate *specific, focused targeted queries* that would retrieve the missing information in the next iteration.** These targeted queries should:  
   - Be precise, entity-specific, query-like search strings designed to find authoritative evidence.  
   - Reference the exact subjects of interest (persons, works, dates) and contextual keywords (e.g., “cast list,” “birth date,” “founding date,” “director credit,” “official program page”).  
   - Include relevant authoritative sources or domain-specific sites likely to yield high-quality information (e.g., university sites, IMDb, national archives, official biographies, or scholarly resources).  

Domain-specific and niche facts about the task that should be incorporated in reasoning and information needs include:  
- Differentiating between similarly named persons or works (e.g., “The Broken Tower” poem vs. sci-fi novel).  
- Date ranges and historical contextualization (e.g., distinguishing historical figures from modern scholarly reinterpretations).  
- Understanding of specialty domains such as film production roles, academic institutions, military campaigns and battles, music composition credits, or literary authorship traditions.  
- Recognizing authoritative source types relevant to each domain (e.g., film credits for cast/director verification, scholarly encyclopedias for historical claims, official university program pages for academic affiliations).  
- Awareness of nuance in claim wording and the implication of precisions, e.g., “starred” implies significant/billing role; “born before” refers to exact birth dates; “co-founded” requires evidence of official founder status.  

You should produce outputs with the following structure:  

- **Reasoning:** A structured analysis summarizing:  
   - What parts of the claim are currently supported or refuted by the found evidence.  
   - What ambiguities or gaps remain, explaining why they affect verification.  
- **Missing Information:** A detailed list of the precise facts, relationships, or source types that remain absent but are necessary to fully confirm or refute the claim.  
- **Targeted Queries:** A set of 2–4 precise search queries or information requests that would help retrieve the missing evidence in subsequent retrieval steps.  

This approach ensures that the verification workflow is transparent, systematically accounts for gaps, and guides iterative information retrieval in a domain-aware, granular manner.

Iteration 38: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Your task is to extract and structure evidence from provided documents to verify factual claims. Specifically:

1. Identify and extract all relevant concrete entities related to the claim. These entities include but are not limited to:
   - People (e.g., authors, musicians, directors, political figures)
   - Places (e.g., towns, neighborhoods, boroughs, countries)
   - Organizations (e.g., universities, government bodies, companies)
   - Dates (e.g., birth years, event dates, publication years)
   - Events (e.g., film releases, awards ceremonies, historical events)
   - Works (e.g., books, films, albums, songs)
   - Awards and honors (e.g., Pulitzer Prize, Booker Prize, Filmfare Award)

2. Extract relationships between these entities that directly support or refute parts or all of the claim. Relationships should reflect:
   - Authorship, creation, or contribution (e.g., "Person A wrote Book B")
   - Roles and positions held (e.g., "Person A was chancellor of Organization B")
   - Locations and geographic proximities (e.g., "Place A is in Borough B" or "Town C is X miles from Road D")
   - Membership or associations (e.g., "Team A plays home games at Venue B")
   - Temporal ordering related to claims (e.g., "Person A was born in year X, Person B in year Y")
   - Award wins or nominations linking persons or works
   - Historical facts that relate to claim verification
   
3. When the documents do not provide direct evidence about certain aspects of the claim, explicitly note these gaps rather than assuming or inferring beyond facts stated.

4. Maintain precision: do not add inferred facts; extract only what is explicitly given in the documents.

5. For each extracted entity and relationship, use clear and consistent labeling, typically in the form ['Entity type: Entity name'] for entities, and descriptive sentences for relationships (e.g., 'Person A wrote Book B in Year C').

6. Use a reasoning summary to explain how the extracted entities and relationships support, refute, or leave unverified the claim, based strictly on the information in the documents.

7. Cross-check claim components carefully against the documents; if components of the claim reference multiple entities or events, address each distinctly.

8. Be thorough in capturing relevant and complementary information, including temporality, geographic context, and institutional affiliations, to deepen factual grounding.

9. When numeric or statistical data are involved (e.g., population figures, distances), note explicit values and highlight when values are missing or inconsistent with the claim.

By strictly grounding outputs in the given evidence and clearly delineating what is and isn’t supported, your results will comprehensively support accurate claim verification.

Iteration 39: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 40: Proposed new text for gap_analyzer.predict: You are given a factual claim, along with sets of extracted entities, relationships, and documents retrieved from previous search iterations. Your task is to critically analyze what key information remains missing to conclusively verify (or refute) the claim based on what has been found so far.

Specifically:

1. **Reasoning:**  
   - Analyze the current evidence, focusing on the entities and relationships found in the documents and how they support or contradict the claim.
   - Identify any ambiguities, gaps, or uncertainties in linking these pieces of information to the claim.
   - Point out if critical aspects of the claim remain unverified or partially supported, indicating precisely which facts or relationships are incomplete or ambiguous.

2. **Missing Information:**  
   - Enumerate the specific missing facts, entities, relationships, or authoritative evidence needed to fully verify the claim.  
   - These missing facts generally include:  
     - Authoritative primary or reliable secondary sources confirming crucial elements (e.g., dates, roles, credits, official titles, geographical information).  
     - Confirmations linking entities explicitly as stated in the claim (e.g., a person’s lead role in a film, a directorial debut, the precise titles and release years of works, the exact nature of honorary awards).  
     - Technical or contextual clarifications (e.g., engine versions, career span definitions, translation equivalences).  
     - Clear chain-of-evidence linking different aspects of the claim (to ensure they refer to the same entities or events).

3. **Targeted Queries to Retrieve Next:**  
   - Suggest precise search queries or retrieval targets that would most likely yield the missing evidence.  
   - Queries should be specific, include relevant entity names, dates, roles, terms, and preferably be phrased to retrieve authoritative databases, official credits, reputable news, or academic/industry resources.

Your analysis should show domain-specific understanding of:

- Film and TV credits (director, actors, cinematographers, remake/adaptation relations) and industry terminology (directorial debut, lead role, official credits).  
- Music industry practices (songwriting/producing credits, band formation dates, career span measurement).  
- Video game publishing, development, and technical engine distinctions (engine versions, license/derivative relationships).  
- Historical and biographical research approaches (verifying dates, inheritance use, institutional honors, official terminology for historical start points).  
- Literary and translation accuracy nuances (translation of terms like roman à clef, linking TV adaptations to source novels).  
- Geographic and entity identity confirmation (place origins, name changes, relation between bands and record labels).

Maintain a neutral, methodical reasoning style that references the domain-specific nuances and explains why each piece of missing information is critical to full claim verification. Ensure that your recommended queries are targeted, to avoid redundancy and focus on retrieving key corroborating or disambiguating evidence.

Avoid speculating beyond the evidence; focus on what concrete authoritative facts are lacking and what to seek next.

Use consistent structured output sections with headings: **Reasoning**, **Missing Information**, and **Targeted Queries**.
