[TIMER] propose_new_texts took 13.05s
Iteration 41: Proposed new text for entity_title_extractor.predict: Task: Extract potential Wikipedia article titles (proper nouns) relevant to a given claim and its retrieved context.

Input format:
- "claim": a natural language statement containing assertions, named entities, or references to people, places, events, organizations, or works.
- "context": supporting text intended to help verify or elaborate on the claim. It may include descriptive passages, disambiguations, or list-like information with multiple entities.

Output format:
- A list of Wikipedia article titles (proper nouns) exactly as they would appear in Wikipedia (including disambiguators in parentheses where applicable, e.g., "Grigori Aleksandrov", "Fort Davis (Alaska)", "The Invisible (band)"), covering the key entities in the claim and context.

Detailed task description and guidelines:

1. Extract entities of the following types that are likely to have standalone Wikipedia articles:
   - People (e.g., full names or common professional names)
   - Places (e.g., cities, countries, administrative divisions, landmarks)
   - Events (e.g., historical events, seasons, award ceremonies)
   - Organizations (e.g., companies, institutions, political parties)
   - Works (e.g., films, albums, songs, books, TV shows)

2. Titles must be exact Wikipedia article titles or the closest likely approximations with standard disambiguation:
   - Use parentheses disambiguators (e.g., "Fort Davis (Alaska)", "The Invisible (band)") when multiple entities share the same base name.
   - Use official names as given in the context if present.
   - Do not alter spelling or rename entities; preserve casing and punctuation as found in reliable sources.

3. Focus on specificity and relevance:
   - Include entities explicitly mentioned in the claim or context that support the claim or provide necessary background.
   - Omit generic terms or descriptive phrases that are not article titles (e.g., "4th largest shopping mall in the world").
   - When a named entity is implicitly referenced but clearly identifiable (e.g., a known historical event or institution), extract the corresponding Wikipedia article title.

4. Include multiple related entities when appropriate:
   - Extract all relevant entities needed to independently identify and verify the claim.
   - For example, include the main person, their works, related organizations, major locations, and significant associated events.

5. Clarify ambiguous entities through context:
   - Use disambiguation information present in the context to assign correct Wikipedia titles.
   - When a name is ambiguous in the claim but clarified in the context, use the disambiguated full title.

6. Exclude:
   - Common nouns or general concepts without clear article title relevance.
   - Entities not directly related to the claim as supported by the context.
   - Non-Wikipedia title descriptors (e.g., song lyrics, generic descriptors).

7. General strategy to apply:
   - Parse claim and context for named entities and proper nouns.
   - Cross-check context for clarifications, disambiguations, and additional relevant entities.
   - Select the minimal set of Wikipedia article titles sufficient to cover the claim and its factual basis in the context.
   - Include persons by their commonly recognized Wikipedia article names.
   - Include works with exact titles and disambiguators as found in the context.
   - Include geographic and organizational entities with standard naming conventions.

8. Aim for precision and completeness balancing:
   - Extract all entities that help establish claim verifiability.
   - Avoid overgeneralization or irrelevant topics.
   - When multiple aliases or closely related entities exist, prefer the most precise and widely accepted Wikipedia article title.

This approach ensures that for fact verification or related downstream tasks, the extracted set directly corresponds to verifiable Wikipedia topics that can support or refute the claim with clarity and accuracy.

Iteration 43: Proposed new text for scorer.scorer.predict: You are given a claim and a document. Your task is to evaluate how relevant the document is to verifying the claim on a scale from 1 to 10, where:

- 10 means the document is highly relevant and provides critical, direct evidence strongly supporting or refuting the claim.
- Scores between 2 and 9 indicate varying degrees of partial, indirect, or limited relevance.
- 1 means the document is completely irrelevant or provides no useful information related to the claim.

To perform this evaluation accurately, follow these guidelines:

1. **Identify Key Elements in the Claim:**  
   Break down the claim into core entities, facts, names, places, dates, works (e.g., films, albums, operas), roles, or any domain-specific terminology mentioned.

2. **Check Document Content Against the Claim:**  
   - Look for explicit mentions of key claim elements in the document (names, titles, dates, roles, places, events, awards, etc.).  
   - Assess whether the document provides information confirming, denying, or clarifying these elements.  
   - Consider whether the document addresses the claim sufficiently to verify or falsify it. If it only partially addresses the claim or offers indirect contextual evidence, reflect this in a moderate score.

3. **Handle Domain-Specific or Niche Information:**  
   The claims and documents often involve specialized knowledge across diverse domains such as:  
   - Entertainment (films, actors, directors, release dates, awards)  
   - Music (albums, musicians, sales figures, genres, collaborations)  
   - Sports (athletes, tournaments, events, results)  
   - History and Politics (historical figures, events, legislative records)  
   - Geography (locations, distances, facility operations)  
   - Religion, Mythology, Science, and other specialized fields  

   Recognize that references may be indirect, or crucial details may be subtle (e.g., an actor’s birthdate, a film’s release year, or a person’s role in an event).

4. **Consider Partial Relevance:**  
   Documents might be relevant in some respects but lack critical details to fully verify the claim (e.g., listing personnel related to a film but missing the specific linkage to the claim). Assign scores reflecting partial but insufficient relevance rather than all-or-nothing.

5. **Use the Scale Consistently Based on Evidence:**  
   - 9-10: Strong, direct, and critical evidence corroborating or refuting the claim.  
   - 5-8: Moderate to fair evidence; the document covers related topics but is missing some key details or does not fully address the claim.  
   - 2-4: Weak or very limited relation, mentions some claim elements but not enough to evaluate thoroughly.  
   - 1: No meaningful mention or connection to claim elements.

6. **Explain Your Reasoning:**  
   Provide a clear rationale referencing specific parts or facts in the document and their relevance or irrelevance to the claim. This explanation should highlight why the document does or does not support verifying the claim.

7. **Avoid Speculation:**  
   Do not infer or assume facts that are not explicitly or clearly indicated in the document. Base your score and reasoning strictly on the content provided.

By applying this approach, you will deliver a consistent, evidence-based relevance score that accurately reflects the degree to which the document supports or contradicts the claim, acknowledging partial and nuanced relevance especially in specialized or niche domains.

Iteration 44: Proposed new text for bridging_identifier.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a claim and a set of retrieved documents related to that claim. Your goal is to identify specific bridging entities—such as people, organizations, events, creative works, or places—that appear in the retrieved documents but are *not explicitly mentioned* in the original claim, yet are essential to verify or refute the claim. These bridging entities serve as intermediate nodes of knowledge or fact that connect different parts of the claim to evidence in the documents.

Bridging entities often fulfill these roles:
- Named individuals (e.g., authors, directors, actors, band members) who clarify vague references in the claim or who contextualize relationships between people and works.
- Organizations or institutions (e.g., record labels, production companies, political parties, educational systems) that link an individual or event to broader structures or bodies relevant to the claim.
- Creative works (albums, films, songs, novels, awards) that directly relate to key aspects of the claim but are unnamed or partially referenced.
- Locations (cities, countries, venues, historic sites) that help disambiguate origins, events, or identities mentioned ambiguously in the claim.
- Historical figures, timelines, or related works that help provide chronological context or factual linkage necessary for verification.

Your process should follow these generalizable strategies:

1. **Understand the Claim’s Components:** Parse the claim into its constituent ideas/entities (persons, works, events), noting ambiguities or missing explicit references.

2. **Analyze the Retrieved Documents:** Identify named entities present in the text that could connect or expand the known elements of the claim. Pay attention to variant names, related persons, associated organizations, or works mentioned.

3. **Identify Missing but Essential Entities:** Determine which entities are crucial to build a chain of evidence for verification yet are absent from the claim itself. These typically include:
   - People whose roles clarify ambiguous references (e.g., the "author of a book" named specifically).
   - Specific albums, films, awards, or events that supply details required to confirm the claim.
   - Parent companies, record labels, or institutional bodies required to verify organizational relationships.
   - Historical or geographical clarifiers necessary for temporal or spatial comparisons.

4. **Include Entities that Will Require Their Own Retrieval:** The entities you identify should be those warranting dedicated, standalone document retrieval because they hold key factual information that completes or supports the verification process.

5. **Avoid Entities Already Explicit in the Claim:** Only suggest bridging entities that appear in the documents but are not explicitly named or directly referenced in the claim.

6. **Provide Clear Reasoning:** When explaining your selection, clearly state why these entities are needed to verify the claim, referencing the relationships or ambiguities they address.

Additional domain knowledge and nuances to consider:

- For biographical or historical claims, bridging entities often include intermediate individuals (e.g., parents, siblings, or collaborators), timelines, or organizations (e.g., political parties, universities).
- For claims about creative works (albums, films, songs), bridging entities may be the specific works themselves, record labels, production companies, or collaborators.
- For claims needing comparison (chronology, origins), bridging entities often include dates, birthplaces, or other contextual figures.
- In the case of ambiguous name references, bridging entities include clarifying similar or related names to avoid confusion.
- When verifying professional roles (director, author, actor), entities connected to filmography, bibliographies, or discographies may be necessary.
- Understanding relationships between works (e.g., sequels, adaptations, remakes) can form necessary bridging entities.
- Corporate hierarchy (parent companies, subsidiaries) is important for verifying ownership or production claims.
- In scientific or philosophical claims, seminal works or related scholars mentioned in documents may be vital bridging points.

Format your response as follows:

- **reasoning:** A brief explanation of why these bridging entities are necessary, referencing the claim and document context.
- **bridging_entities:** A list of entity names (people, works, organizations, places, etc.) found in the documents but not mentioned explicitly in the claim, which warrant their own retrieval for verification.

Your output should help guide an information retrieval system to automatically fetch detailed documents for these intermediate entities, creating a comprehensive chain of evidence for the original claim’s verification.

Iteration 45: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 45: Proposed new text for bridging_identifier.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a claim and a set of retrieved documents related to that claim. Your goal is to identify specific bridging entities—such as people, organizations, events, creative works, or places—that appear in the retrieved documents but are *not explicitly mentioned* in the original claim, yet are essential to verify or refute the claim. These bridging entities serve as intermediate nodes of knowledge or fact that connect different parts of the claim to evidence in the documents.

Bridging entities often fulfill these roles:
- Named individuals (e.g., authors, directors, actors, band members) who clarify vague references in the claim or who contextualize relationships between people and works.
- Organizations or institutions (e.g., record labels, production companies, political parties, educational systems) that link an individual or event to broader structures or bodies relevant to the claim.
- Creative works (albums, films, songs, novels, awards) that directly relate to key aspects of the claim but are unnamed or partially referenced.
- Locations (cities, countries, venues, historic sites) that help disambiguate origins, events, or identities mentioned ambiguously in the claim.
- Historical figures, timelines, or related works that help provide chronological context or factual linkage necessary for verification.

Your process should follow these generalizable strategies:

1. **Understand the Claim’s Components:** Parse the claim into its constituent ideas/entities (persons, works, events), noting ambiguities or missing explicit references.

2. **Analyze the Retrieved Documents:** Identify named entities present in the text that could connect or expand the known elements of the claim. Pay attention to variant names, related persons, associated organizations, or works mentioned.

3. **Identify Missing but Essential Entities:** Determine which entities are crucial to build a chain of evidence for verification yet are absent from the claim itself. These typically include:
   - People whose roles clarify ambiguous references (e.g., the "author of a book" named specifically).
   - Specific albums, films, awards, or events that supply details required to confirm the claim.
   - Parent companies, record labels, or institutional bodies required to verify organizational relationships.
   - Historical or geographical clarifiers necessary for temporal or spatial comparisons.

4. **Include Entities that Will Require Their Own Retrieval:** The entities you identify should be those warranting dedicated, standalone document retrieval because they hold key factual information that completes or supports the verification process.

5. **Avoid Entities Already Explicit in the Claim:** Only suggest bridging entities that appear in the documents but are not explicitly named or directly referenced in the claim.

6. **Provide Clear Reasoning:** When explaining your selection, clearly state why these entities are needed to verify the claim, referencing the relationships or ambiguities they address.

Additional domain knowledge and nuances to consider:

- For biographical or historical claims, bridging entities often include intermediate individuals (e.g., parents, siblings, or collaborators), timelines, or organizations (e.g., political parties, universities).
- For claims about creative works (albums, films, songs), bridging entities may be the specific works themselves, record labels, production companies, or collaborators.
- For claims needing comparison (chronology, origins), bridging entities often include dates, birthplaces, or other contextual figures.
- In the case of ambiguous name references, bridging entities include clarifying similar or related names to avoid confusion.
- When verifying professional roles (director, author, actor), entities connected to filmography, bibliographies, or discographies may be necessary.
- Understanding relationships between works (e.g., sequels, adaptations, remakes) can form necessary bridging entities.
- Corporate hierarchy (parent companies, subsidiaries) is important for verifying ownership or production claims.
- In scientific or philosophical claims, seminal works or related scholars mentioned in documents may be vital bridging points.

Format your response as follows:

- **reasoning:** A brief explanation of why these bridging entities are necessary, referencing the claim and document context.
- **bridging_entities:** A list of entity names (people, works, organizations, places, etc.) found in the documents but not mentioned explicitly in the claim, which warrant their own retrieval for verification.

Your output should help guide an information retrieval system to automatically fetch detailed documents for these intermediate entities, creating a comprehensive chain of evidence for the original claim’s verification.

Iteration 46: Proposed new text for scorer.scorer.predict: Task: Evaluate the relevance of a given document to a specific claim by providing a relevance score from 1 to 10, where 10 indicates that the document is highly relevant and provides critical, direct evidence supporting or refuting the claim, and 1 indicates the document is completely irrelevant with no useful information for verifying the claim.

Input Format:
- A claim: a factual or knowledge-based statement containing named entities (people, films, songs, companies, events, etc.), dates, places, or other domain-specific details.
- A document: a piece of text (e.g., short biography, film synopsis, company description, list entry) that may or may not contain information pertinent to verifying the claim.

Detailed Task Description and Guidance:
1. Identify key entities and facts within the claim (such as specific names, dates, titles, organizations, events) that need verification.

2. Analyze the document thoroughly for any mention of these key claim elements or closely related terms/synonyms, paying attention to:
   - Named entities (people, places, films, songs, companies, events)
   - Relevant dates (e.g., years of film release, birth dates)
   - Roles or relationships (e.g., who directed a film, who acted in it)
   - Specific detailed facts mentioned in the claim.

3. Assess the extent and quality of evidence the document provides:
   - Direct confirmations or contradictions of the claim's components merit higher relevance scores.
   - Indirect or partial evidence supporting parts of the claim may warrant moderate scores.
   - Documents with only broad contextual or tangential relations to the claim deserve low relevance scores.
   - Documents entirely unrelated to the claim’s content or entities receive the lowest score (1).

4. Avoid conflating similar but distinct entities (e.g., similarly named people with different professions) unless the document clearly links them.

5. Consider the scope of the claim’s content—one or multiple specific facts—and evaluate if the document confirms, partially confirms, or fails to confirm them.

6. If the document mentions related entities or contexts but lacks direct information about the core claim elements, score should reflect only minimal or no relevance.

7. The evaluation is binary in terms of relevance (relevant vs irrelevant) but uses a fine-grained 1-10 scale:
   - 10: Provides critical and sufficient evidence directly supporting or disproving the claim.
   - 7-9: Provides significant, though not complete, evidence related to the claim.
   - 4-6: Partial or indirect evidence related to some aspects of the claim.
   - 2-3: Only vaguely or tangentially related information.
   - 1: Completely irrelevant; no mention or connection to claim entities or facts.

8. Do not rely on external knowledge or assumptions beyond what the document provides.

Deliverables:
- A concise reasoning paragraph explaining the relevance assessment based on the above criteria.
- A numerical relevance score (1-10) reflecting the degree of relevance of the document to the claim.

Domain-Specific Notes:
- Film and television: Verify actors, directors, film titles, release years, relation between films, and adaptation sources.
- Music: Verify songwriters, performers, instrument roles, music groups, and related events or venues.
- Sports: Verify teams, leagues, player relations, dates of birth, roles, and associations.
- Companies and organizations: Verify headquarters locations, founders, operational dates, and relationships with other entities.
- Biographical data: Verify individuals’ roles, birth/death dates, professions, and related historical facts.
- Be cautious with similarly named entities and distinct concepts (e.g., different airports with similar codes, different films with related subjects).
- Claims often involve compositional relationships (e.g., "X is the director of film Y which starred actor Z"), and verification depends on document mentioning these relationships explicitly.

Overall, apply careful entity matching and evidence validation strictly limited to information presented in the document to produce an objective, evidence-based relevance scoring.

Iteration 47: Proposed new text for scorer.scorer.predict: You are tasked with evaluating the relevance of a given document to a specified claim. Your response should be a relevance score between 1 and 10, where:

- 10 means the document is highly relevant and provides critical, direct evidence that supports or refutes the claim in detail.
- 1 means the document is completely irrelevant or provides no useful information to assess the claim.

When evaluating relevance, consider the following:

1. **Direct Evidence:** The document should explicitly mention key entities, facts, or relationships involved in the claim. Partial relevance (e.g., mentioning part of the claim context but not the entities involved) should receive a moderate score, reflecting the degree to which the document could help verify or falsify the claim.

2. **Indirect or Partial Relevance:** Some documents might provide context or partial information related to the claim (e.g., details about one entity involved but not others, background information, dates, locations). These are less relevant than documents that fully address the claim but more relevant than documents completely unrelated.

3. **Irrelevance:** If the document covers a topic unrelated to the claim, does not mention any key elements of the claim, or does not provide any evidence pertinent to verifying or falsifying the claim, assign a score of 1.

4. **Domain-Specific Knowledge:** The claims may involve niche, domain-specific facts—such as film directors, actors associated with specific movies or roles, sports players with match or birthdate information, works of literature and their authors, musical albums and artist associations, historical events or places, and other cultural and media references. Pay close attention to:

   - Proper names and aliases (e.g., actors, directors, musicians, fictional characters)
   - Specific works referenced (films, TV series, albums, operas, books)
   - Dates and timelines relevant to the claim
   - Relationships (e.g., who directed/produced/acted in a film, who authored a book, birth years)
   - Geographic locations as they relate to claims about populations or events

5. **Evaluating Partial Matches:** If the document confirms components of the claim but misses others (for example, confirms the existence of a related film but not casting, or mentions a work by a named author but omits critical details about awards or dates), provide an intermediate relevance score that reflects this partial informativeness.

6. **Reasoning:** Although you will only provide a score, ensure your internal reasoning weighs how clearly the document supports or contradicts the claim, how complete or direct the evidence is, and whether it addresses the core factual elements necessary for claim verification.

Input format:

- **claim:** A single factual statement or complex claim that often involves relationships or comparisons between named entities, works, or facts.
- **document:** A passage describing information that may or may not pertain to the claim. Could be a biography, synopsis, description of a work, historical data, etc.

Your task:

- Assess whether the document contains factually relevant content to the claim.
- Assign a numerical relevance score from 1 (completely irrelevant) to 10 (fully relevant and critical evidence).
- When partial relevance exists, proportionally scale the score.

Keep in mind from prior examples that domain expert knowledge of cultural and media entities, as well as precise matching of claim elements to document content, is vital to proper scoring.

Iteration 49: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim that involves multiple linked factual assertions spanning one or more domains such as geography, biography, media, history, products, or others. Your goal is to accurately decompose the original claim into 2-3 distinct, clear, and independently verifiable sub-questions. Each sub-question should isolate one specific fact or logical “hop” necessary to evaluate the claim’s truthfulness step-by-step.

Guidelines and domain-specific details to ensure high-quality decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims require discrete verification steps:**  
   - Identify each distinct factual or logical element (“hop”) in the claim that must be established or refuted.  
   - Formulate one sub-question per hop to enable independent fact-checking. Multiple facts combined in a single sub-question reduce clarity and verifiability.

2. **Disambiguate ambiguous or vague references:**  
   - Explicitly clarify ambiguous entities (e.g., people with common names, generic event titles, festivals with multiple versions, or roles in films) by asking for identifiers such as full names, dates, locations, or official titles.  
   - When claims mention pronouns or unclear descriptors ("the actress," "the company"), specify the intended entity by connecting to a unique identifier or role.

3. **Separate compound or conjunctive claims:**  
   - If the claim includes conjunctions (e.g., “both A and B”), create independent questions to verify each component separately.  
   - When the claim involves relationships (e.g., “brother of X,” “director of film Y”), one question should verify the relationship/entity identity, another to verify the linked fact.

4. **Domains and typical fact types to cover:**  
   - **Geography:** Locations served by transportation hubs, official designations or changes over time, geographic relationships (“just under four miles”) and coordinates.  
   - **Media & Film:** Cast and crew credits (director, actors, stunt performers), release years, premiere events, roles played, production nationalities, filmographies and debuts, awards and nominations, franchise specifics (e.g., Disney’s nth animated feature).  
   - **Biographical & Relationships:** Birthdates, familial relationships, career chronology, honorary degrees or affiliations.  
   - **Product & Design:** Company makers and inventors, product line specifics, comparative popularity or market data, manufacturer nationalities.  
   - **Historical Events:** Official place names over historical periods, political offices held (including dates and districts), historical military hardware and their manufacturers, replacements in service.  
   - **Branding & Marketing:** Slogans and their creators, logos associated with slogans, valuations specifying sources and dates.

5. **Focus on verifiable, well-documented facts:**  
   - Formulate questions that can be answered by authoritative or easily accessible sources such as official biographies, film credits, brand valuation reports, festival archives, government records, or publication mastheads.

6. **Use precise, neutral, and unambiguous wording:**  
   - Avoid loaded, leading, or vague phrasing.  
   - Reflect the original claim’s content directly in your sub-questions without assumptions beyond clarifying ambiguity.

7. **Example of reasoning approach to decomposition:**  
   - Identify the core entities referenced (persons, places, films, organizations).  
   - Clarify ambiguous references (e.g., which version of a festival, which film adaptation, which edition of a product).  
   - Separate the fact-checkable properties or relationships into individual questions (e.g., birthdates, career roles, production credits, event dates).  
   - If the claim contains comparisons, ask for data about each subject independently for clear later comparison.

8. **Address all parts of the claim:**  
   - Ensure the set of sub-questions fully covers every piece of the claim to allow comprehensive verification.  
   - When facts are sequential or relational, structure sub-questions to first identify entities, then verify their attributes or relationships.

By adhering to these instructions, you will produce modular, transparent sub-questions that enable stepwise and fact-based verification or falsification of any complex multi-hop claim.

Output format expected:

- A list of 2-3 clear, specific, independently verifiable sub-questions, each targeting one unique fact or logical step essential to validate the original claim.

Iteration 50: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be presented with a complex multi-hop claim — a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of factual or logical “hops.” Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific, focused sub-questions, each isolating one unique fact, relationship, or verification step essential to confirming or refuting the overall claim.

Your sub-questions must be precisely crafted, independently verifiable, and clearly target one factual piece or logical step necessary for fact-checking. They should disambiguate ambiguous terms, separate conjunctions or comparative claims, and clarify references to persons, places, works, or events to ensure each question is straightforward and can be answered from authoritative, well-documented sources such as official filmographies, award records, biographies, production credits, or geographic data.

Key Points and Domain-Specific Guidance:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These often combine various entities, relationships, or attributes across multiple domains, including but not limited to:
   - Film, television, and media (e.g., director and producer credits, release years, character names, premiere dates).
   - Music (e.g., recording artists, albums, song covers, music awards).
   - Literature and theater (e.g., authorship of novels or plays, publication dates, award nominations).
   - Geography and administrative data (e.g., city or country origins, airport codes, railway station service areas).
   - Biographical and relational facts (e.g., birth dates and places, family or professional relationships, career timelines).
   - Corporate or organizational structures (e.g., shareholdings in companies, founders, operational bases).
   - Awards and honors (e.g., specifying the awarding organization, categories, years of nomination or winning).
   - Music genres, voice types, and artistic roles.

2. **Decompose logically and factually**:
   - Separate each important fact or “hop” into its own question.
   - When the claim involves comparative or conjunction statements, ask separate questions for each conjunct or comparative element.
   - For relational or possessive claims, isolate identity questions (“Who is X?”) from associated factual claims (“Did X do Y?”).
   - When the claim bundles multiple facts in one sentence (e.g., someone wrote and directed a film in a certain language), split these into distinct questions to verify authorship, directorship, and language independently.

3. **Disambiguate ambiguous terms and references**:
   - Clarify ambiguous names or titles by including full names, dates, or additional context (e.g., distinguish between multiple films with the same title, versions of a song, or different people with similar names).
   - Identify potential misspellings or alternative spellings (e.g., “Charpes Lane” likely refers to “Charles Lane”).
   - When a person or entity is referenced indirectly (e.g., “the creator of X”), form a clarifying question that identifies the exact person/entity before verifying linked facts.

4. **Use precise, neutral, and verifiable wording**:
   - Do not presume any part of the claim is true—rather, ask to confirm or disprove with facts.
   - Frame questions to request specific data points or documented sources (e.g., “What are the production credits?”, “Who held ownership shares as of a certain year?”, “How many titles were won?”, “What is the place of origin?”).
   - Avoid loaded or subjective language; keep questions factual and clear.

5. **Consider timelines and operational status**:
   - For events or statuses that can change over time (e.g., shareholdings, operational bases, memberships), specify dates or historical context where helpful.
   - For accomplishments or career milestones, specify periods or total counts up to a given date.

6. **Examples of domain-specific details to consider in sub-questions**:
   - Film/TV: director, producer, cast names; release years; premiere networks/dates.
   - Music: artist or band origin; album sales figures; song authorship; cover versions by specified artists at particular dates.
   - Theater/literature: authorship dates; production casts; award nominations for specific categories.
   - Geography: latitude/longitude; city/country origin; distance measures.
   - Corporate: stakes and ownership percentages with documented time frames; founders and their roles.
   - Awards: precise name of awards, nominating body, and year.
   - Biographical: birth dates and places; career timelines; relationship clarification.
   - Voice types/vocal ranges: documented roles or voice classifications compared within a group.

7. **Stepwise Verification Structure**:
   - Your sub-questions should enable a stepwise breakdown that either validates or refutes individual parts of the claim, helping identify which parts hold or fail.
   - This modularization allows transparent, evidence-based fact-checking.

Input Format:

- Claim: A natural language complex factual claim potentially involving multiple linked facts.

Output Format:

- A reasoning summary briefly outlining the decomposition logic.
- A list of 2-3 independently verifiable sub-questions formulated as neutral, precise inquiries targeting each key factual hop.

Your goal is to facilitate robust verification by transforming opaque, compound claims into clear, focused, and unambiguous fact-checking questions based on detailed domain knowledge and best practices in multi-hop fact decomposition.

Iteration 52: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: You are given a complex, multi-hop factual claim that requires verification by gathering and comparing multiple distinct pieces of information.

Your task is to decompose the claim into 2-3 clear, specific sub-questions that:

- Each target a distinct piece of information necessary to verify a component of the claim,  
- Break down linked assertions or multi-step chains of reasoning into independently answerable factual queries,  
- Clarify any ambiguous or domain-specific terms and specify precise entities, roles, or measurable attributes (e.g., number of awards, identity of people/groups, dates, locations, etc.),  
- Explicitly separate comparative or relational steps (e.g., comparing award counts, locations, birth dates) into focused queries for each item involved,  
- Anchor questions to well-defined, commonly accepted datasets or metrics when applicable (e.g., specifying types of awards, roster lineups at specific times, census data sources),  
- Include clarifying contextual details to avoid ambiguous references (e.g., disambiguating homonyms, specifying the exact entity or event referenced),  
- For ambiguous or potentially conflated references, either resolve them in the questions or note the ambiguity explicitly to enable precise fact retrieval,  
- Avoid mixing multiple distinct facts into a single question; instead, split into separate questions where each involves one or two closely linked factual elements,  
- Structure questions to facilitate independent verification of each fact or assertion in the claim, so the final determination can be composed from the collected answers.

Additionally, if the claim involves entities from specialized domains (e.g., sports, music industry, historical battles, awards, geography), incorporate known conventions for those domains, such as:

- Using well-defined milestones (e.g., ATP titles, Grand Slam wins for tennis players; award names and years for arts; population estimates sources for demographics),  
- Identifying individuals with full names and roles, birth/death dates, or familial relationships as relevant,  
- Ensuring questions precisely capture time-dependent attributes (e.g., lineups or memberships at specific times, award nominations or wins in particular years).

Deliver your output as a list of 2-3 succinct, clearly phrased sub-questions explicitly designed to verify the claim, mindful of nuance, precision, and unambiguous fact-checking.

Example guiding principles to follow:

- When a comparative claim involves quantities (e.g., number of awards or members), ask separately for each party’s number based on a clearly defined metric.  
- When a claim involves an association (e.g., someone belonging to an institution or being related to another person), verify the identity and the relationship or role independently.  
- When a claim involves geographic assertions (e.g., locations, distances, jurisdictions), isolate confirmation of each geographical fact and measure or data source as needed.  
- When claims involve entity attributes over time (e.g., award nominations, visits, releases), specify the relevant timeframe.  
- When claims involve ambiguities or multiple possible interpretations, incorporate clarifying sub-questions or indicate which interpretation is used.

Your decomposition should prepare for straightforward evidence retrieval and credible, stepwise verification of the multi-hop claim.

Iteration 53: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Task Description:
You are given a claim and a set of retrieved documents relevant to verifying that claim. Your task is to extract and organize all key concrete entities and their relationships from the documents that support or contradict the claim. These extractions will be used to verify the claim’s factual accuracy.

Detailed Instructions:

1. Input Understanding:
   - The input consists of:
     a) A textual claim — a factual statement which may contain multiple assertions.
     b) A set of retrieved documents containing factual information, descriptions, or biographies relevant to the claim.
   - The documents may include people, places, organizations, dates, events, works (films, books, songs), awards, roles, and so forth.

2. Output Requirements:
   Produce three outputs:
   a) Reasoning: A concise, logically structured explanation summarizing the evidence in the documents that support or contradict the claim. Your reasoning must:
      - Align directly with the facts extracted from the documents.
      - Highlight both supporting and contradictory points.
      - Clearly indicate when documents lack information to confirm specific parts of the claim.
      - Verify temporal consistency (e.g., dates must be plausible).
      - Interpret linked entity relationships (e.g., person A acted in film B; film B is an adaptation of novel C).
   
   b) Entities: List all relevant concrete entities discovered in the documents that pertain to the claim, in the format:
      - 'Person: Full Name (birth/death dates if available)'
      - 'Film: Title (release year)'
      - 'Book/Novel: Title (publication year)'
      - 'Song: Title (release year)'
      - 'Organization: Name'
      - 'Date: YYYY' or specific dates
      - 'Role/Character: Name'
      - 'Place/Location: Name'
      - Other domain-specific entities (e.g., Award, Style, Event)
   Include birth/death years or relevant dates when available to clarify identities.

   c) Relationships: Extract all explicit or logically inferable relationships between the entities that are relevant to verifying the claim, for example:
      - Person A appeared in Film B (year)
      - Person A directed Film B
      - Person A wrote Book C
      - Film B is an adaptation of Book C by Author D
      - Person A played Character C in Film B
      - Film B was released in Year Y
      - Person A born on Date D
      - Event E took place at Location L on Date D
      - Person A is of Nationality N
   Format each as a sentence or simple phrase connecting entities and factual data.

3. Domain and Factual Nuances:
   - Distinguish between persons with similar or identical names by birthdates, professions, or other clarifiers.
   - Pay attention to temporal data ensuring event/year consistency with the claim.
   - Identify if a person is fictional or real; for fictional characters, specify the work they appear in.
   - Clarify adaptations and derivative works: when a film, TV show or song is adapted from another work, accurately connect entities.
   - Interpret nuanced claims around professions, collaborations, or influence (e.g., two directors sharing roles).
   - Capture distinctions such as professional careers, notable roles, or temporal overlaps.
   - Identify indirect links (e.g., actor A played character B in a show inspired by film C).
   - Note misstatements in the claim if contradicted by factual timelines or lack of evidence.
   
4. General Strategy:
   - Carefully cross-reference dates, names, and events across multiple documents.
   - Track entities across different document passages to ensure comprehensive coverage.
   - Avoid introducing info not present in documents.
   - When evidence contradicts or partially supports the claim, explicitly note this in reasoning.
   - Do not assume facts beyond the document content.
   - Focus on relevance to the claim to avoid unnecessary entity clutter.
   
5. Avoid:
   - Unsupported assumptions about entity details.
   - Speculation about missing data.
   - Mixing unrelated persons or works with similar names.
   - Ignoring temporal conflicts.

Summary:
Your role is to distill and synthesize factually precise, entity-specific evidence from multiple documents into a structured format that clearly supports or refutes the claim. This involves detailed named entity recognition, relationship extraction, and contextual reasoning drawing upon the documents provided.

Iteration 54: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 55: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Your task is to extract key entities and their relationships from provided documents that are relevant for verifying a given claim. 

Specifically, you must:

1. Identify and list concrete, domain-relevant entities such as:
   - People (including actors, directors, musicians, politicians, historical figures, scientists, etc.)
   - Places (cities, states, countries, specific locations, buildings, landmarks)
   - Organizations (companies, universities, political parties, bands, production companies)
   - Dates (years, exact dates, event timelines)
   - Events (historical events, film releases, awards, important occurrences)
   - Creative works (films, songs, albums, books, video games, artworks)
   - Other domain-specific entities mentioned clearly in the documents (e.g., airports, railway stations, aircraft, political offices)

2. Extract explicit relationships between these entities that directly support verifying the claim. These relationships should be factual and include:
   - Roles and affiliations (e.g., "Person X directed Film Y", "Person A is chair of Organization B")
   - Temporal relations (e.g., release dates, operation periods, participation timelines)
   - Family or social relationships when relevant (e.g., marriage, adoption)
   - Geographical containment or proximity (e.g., "Place A is located in Place B", "Airport C is adjacent to Campus D")
   - Works and contributions (e.g., "Person X wrote Song Y", "Game Z features magic using Candy A")
   - Notable facts supporting or refuting claims (e.g., awards received, organizational ownership)

3. Provide supporting facts referencing the documents (by key and index) to anchor your extracted facts and reasoning. 

4. When a claim references implicit information (e.g., a species is a flowering plant), confirm that the document provides explicit taxonomy or definition; if not, state the missing evidence.

5. Maintain clarity and specificity; include relevant dates or numeric details when provided.

6. Use the following strategy:
   - First, locate and confirm entities mentioned in the claim within the documents.
   - Then, identify any direct relationships or explicit statements linking those entities to each other or supporting the claim.
   - Note alternative names, titles, and dates to ensure correct entity alignment.
   - Highlight any missing or ambiguous information that prevents verification.

7. Do not infer or introduce knowledge not found in the documents. When parts of the claim cannot be corroborated by the documents, explicitly state which evidence is missing.

8. Output the following:
   - A concise reasoning summary explaining how the entities and their relationships support or negate the claim.
   - A list of key entities identified (using a clear label for type and name).
   - A list of extracted relationships (in the form "[Entity 1] [relation] [Entity 2 or attribute]").

This process enables accurate fact verification grounded strictly in the provided textual evidence across diverse domains such as film, music, politics, history, academia, geography, sports, and science.

Iteration 56: Proposed new text for gap_analyzer.predict: Task Description:

You are tasked with critically analyzing ongoing claim verification processes. Given a claim and the current evidence and findings (entities found, relationships identified, and documents retrieved), you must determine what essential information remains missing that prevents full verification of the claim.

Your detailed responsibilities per iteration are:
1. Analyze the given claim in light of the entities, relationships, and documents found so far.
2. Identify and articulate the specific missing critical facts, entities, relationships, or documentary evidence required to verify or refute the claim conclusively.
3. Highlight any ambiguities or confusions in the current evidence that prevent closing the verification loop (e.g., ambiguous references, network mismatches, publication date uncertainties, or conflations between entities like persons vs organizations).
4. Formulate precise, targeted queries or information requests that will help retrieve the missing pieces during the next retrieval iteration of evidence.

Domain-specific/factual knowledge and considerations:

- Identification and disambiguation of entities is crucial. For example, clarifying if an acronym like "NMIO" refers to a known entity or is a mistaken alias; separating persons with similar names; and differentiating between organizations, locations, or objects that share similar identifiers.

- Claims often hinge on hierarchical and temporal relationships: e.g., release dates of films, publication years of games, birth dates of persons, or historical development origins of dog breeds. Knowledge of standardized identifiers (e.g., IATA/ICAO codes for airports, network broadcasters for TV series) assists in precise entity matching.

- Multiple similar or cousin entities may be referenced. A missing clarification of exactly which entity (e.g., which "cousin" dog breed) the claim centers on leads to incomplete verification.

- Distinguishing between network broadcast platforms (Zee TV vs Star Plus), or production details (disambiguating films vs miniseries with similar names) is often needed.

- Confirmation from authoritative sources is critical. These include studio or league official pages, encyclopedias or well-established databases (e.g., IMDb for films, official sports league sites for memberships and sponsorships, the Central Statistics Office for population data, or recognized dictionaries for terminology).

- Discrepancies in dates (e.g., film release years, company bankruptcies) and entity statuses (e.g., airports operational status) must be resolved with direct references reiterating exact dates, roles, or credits.

- Differentiating between similar-sounding or similarly abbreviated entities (e.g., National Maritime College of Ireland vs National Maritime Intelligence-Integration Office) avoids erroneous conclusions.

Generalizable strategy:

- Systematically compare claim components against retrieved facts, noting matches and contradictions.

- Identify ambiguity or lack of specificity in entity references within the claim.

- Prioritize the identification of primary sources or authoritative secondary sources for each critical fact.

- Generate missing information as explicit factual questions or data needed to close verification gaps.

- Suggest targeted, precise search queries or information requests for the next iteration to fill the identified gaps.

Input format:

You will be provided structured inputs including:
- The claim text.
- Entities found so far, with types and attributes.
- Relationships identified among these entities.
- Number and nature of documents retrieved so far.

Output requirements:

Return:
- A detailed reasoning paragraph explaining what is missing and why.
- A list of specific missing information or factoids.
- A list of targeted queries or data requests for the next retrieval.

This enables an iterative evidence-gathering and claim-verification workflow.

Your output should help focus the next retrieval step on critical missing elements to move the claim verification process forward efficiently and authoritatively.

Each query should approach the claim from a unique angle to find supporting documents that might be missed by a single query.

Perspectives to consider:
- Entity-focused: queries targeting specific people, places, or organizations mentioned
- Relationship-focused: queries about connections and interactions between entities
- Temporal-focused: queries about time periods, dates, or chronological aspects
- Comparison-focused: queries comparing or contrasting different aspects of the claim
- Context-focused: queries about broader context or background information

Iteration 58: Proposed new text for perspective_generator.predict: Task: Given a claim and its relevant context, generate 4-5 diverse, well-targeted query reformulations to maximize retrieval coverage for verifying or refuting the claim. 

The key purpose is to create queries that approach the claim from multiple distinct perspectives to comprehensively cover relevant documents/facts that might otherwise be missed by a single query. Queries should be precise, factually grounded, and designed to find supporting evidence or counter-evidence within the supplied or related knowledge sources.

Input Format:
- Claim: A statement or assertion to be verified or refuted.
- Context: Background information or relevant documents which may contain supporting or contradicting facts related to the claim.

Expected Output:
- A set of 4-5 queries, each crafted from a uniquely different perspective focusing on a distinct conceptual angle.
- Each query should clearly be phrased to focus on one dimension or type of information useful for checking evidence underlying the claim.

Perspectives to cover (each query should target one or more of these, ensuring maximal diversity):
1. Entity-focused: Queries focusing on the main people, organizations, places, or named entities directly mentioned or involved in the claim. Such queries seek factual info on these entities (e.g., biographies, headquarters, ownership, roles).
2. Relationship-focused: Queries designed to investigate relationships, interactions, and connections between entities (e.g., ownership, collaborations, roles in events or works, comparative roles).
3. Temporal-focused: Queries emphasizing timing, dates, sequence, or chronology relevant to the claim (e.g., dates of events, releases, life spans, acquisition timelines).
4. Comparison-focused: Queries that contrast or compare entities, events or attributes to clarify distinctions or rankings (e.g., comparing corporate headquarters locations, award recipients, years, or rankings).
5. Context-focused: Broader queries that explore the general background, historical or thematic context to clarify ambiguous or implicit aspects of the claim (e.g., what is the broader theme of a media work, cultural background of a group, classification issues, or domain-specific definitions).

Approach & Reasoning Guidance:
- For each query, identify the knowledge gaps or ambiguities in the claim or context that must be resolved to verify the claim.
- Issue queries to confirm or disconfirm specific facts (e.g., entity biographies, event participation, award winners, production details, dates).
- Use queries that explicitly target key claim components: verifying entity identities, roles, dates, hierarchical or causal relationships, and any possible confusion due to similarly named items.
- Account for potential ambiguity in the claim (e.g., temporal ambiguity, different possible entity referents, multiple similarly titled works).
- Avoid overlap: each query should explore a unique angle rather than restate similar queries.

Domain-Specific Considerations Evident from Examples:
- Claims often combine biographical, corporate, historical, or media-related information, requiring queries across distinct expert domains (e.g., entertainment, botany, military history, linguistics).
- Timelines and corporate relationships matter deeply: queries often involve acquisition histories, headquarters relocations, dates of events or releases.
- Entity disambiguation is crucial: multiple persons or works may share similar names or titles.
- Understanding of specific domain terminology is necessary for effective queries (e.g., "bass" voice range in music, "family name" in biological taxonomy, "uncontacted" tribes in anthropology).
- Queries should target production credits, heritage and origins, and relevant media/factual milestones.
- Queries also often need to check contradiction or correction of claims (e.g., checking incorrect affiliations or factual errors).

Instructions for the Assistant:
1. Carefully analyze the claim and supplied context to identify key entities, relationships, temporal markers, or potentially ambiguous elements.
2. From this analysis, define 4-5 distinct perspectives as above.
3. Formulate concise, specific queries for each perspective that would retrieve evidence for or against the claim.
4. Clearly indicate the focus of each query in terms of entity, relationship, temporal, comparison, or context.
5. Make sure no two queries target the same fact or angle redundantly.
6. If the claim is ambiguous or could be interpreted multiple ways, at least one query should address that ambiguity.
7. The queries should stand alone and contain relevant keywords to guide retrieval (e.g., include specific entity names, dates, locations).
8. Aim to cover all critical fact checks that would enable a human or machine to verify or refute the claim given retrieved documents.

Iteration 59: Proposed new text for entity_extractor.predict: Task Description:
You are given a claim and a set of retrieved documents intended to verify that claim. Your task is to extract all key concrete entities and relationships from the retrieved documents that help verify or refute the claim. Focus specifically on identifying concrete, verifiable information about entities such as people, places, organizations, dates, events, works of art (songs, albums, films, books, operas, TV shows), and their explicit connections or relationships mentioned in the documents.

Guidelines:

1. Identify and extract key entities:
   - People (including their full names, birth/death dates if available, roles, occupations)
   - Places (cities, neighborhoods, airports, landmarks)
   - Organizations (companies, production studios, schools, government bodies)
   - Dates (years, specific dates, time periods)
   - Events (historical events, performances, premieres)
   - Works (films, TV shows, operas, albums, songs, literary works)
   - Other concrete objects relevant to the claim (monuments, artworks, characters)

2. Extract relationships between entities that are explicitly supported by the document texts. These include:
   - Authorship, composition, or creation (e.g., who composed a song, wrote a book, directed a film)
   - Collaborations and personal relationships (e.g., spouse, marriage, mentorship, co-creators)
   - Performances or roles (e.g., actor portraying a character, singer performing a song)
   - Organizational roles or memberships (e.g., who founded a company, who directs an organization)
   - Locations associated with entities or events (e.g., where a school district applies, where an event occurred)
   - Dates associated with events or entities (e.g., birth dates, release dates, founding dates)
   - Historical timelines and successions (e.g., who ruled when, who succeeded whom)
   - Other relevant, verifiable associations (e.g., which choir performed which composition)

3. Reasoning:
   - Use explicit information only; do not assume or speculate about entities or relationships not supported by the documents.
   - If key elements of the claim or the entities it references are missing in the documents, clearly state that verification is not possible.
   - Where applicable, tie multiple pieces of document evidence together to support veracity or identify gaps in support.

4. Format your output with the following:
   - Reasoning narrative: Summarize how the documents relate to the claim, what is supported or missing.
   - Entities: A comprehensive list of extracted concrete entities with relevant descriptors.
   - Relationships: A clear list of factual relationships among entities, as explicitly stated or documented in the texts.

Domain-specific facts and terminologies to be aware of (gleaned from examples):
- Nomenclature and role attribution for historical figures, political leaders, composers, and artists.
- Recognition of co-emperors and dynastic successions in Roman history (e.g., Nerva–Antonine dynasty).
- Understanding theatrical, musical, and cinematic works, including creators, cast, and productions.
- Geographic and administrative divisions (e.g., NYC Community Boards, school districts).
- Industry-specific organizations and units within companies (e.g., Walt Disney Imagineering, Walt Disney Creative Entertainment).
- Naming conventions and distinctions between characters, actors, and roles in entertainment.
- Knowledge that artistic collaborations often include spouses or recurring partners (e.g., composers and librettists).
- Historical events with their corresponding dates and significance to the claim.

Generalizable Strategy:
- Extract entities systematically by scanning for named people, places, organizations, dates, and works.
- Identify the explicit relationships by reading for verbs and prepositions indicating connections (e.g., "wrote", "directed", "born in", "performed by").
- Cross-reference entities with the claim components to identify which document facts are relevant to supporting or refuting the claim.
- Note explicitly when an element of the claim is missing or unsupported in the documents.
- Organize information clearly to assist downstream fact verification, keeping a focus on concrete, unambiguous facts.

This instruction aims to ensure comprehensive, factually precise, and domain-aware extraction of entities and relationships to assess claim veracity based solely on the provided documents.

Iteration 60: Proposed new text for claim_decomposer.predict: Task Description:

You will be given a complex multi-hop claim—a statement that connects multiple factual assertions or involves several linked pieces of information. Your task is to decompose this claim into 2-3 distinct, specific sub-questions that can each be answered independently to verify or refute the overall claim. Each sub-question should target one unique fact or logical “hop” necessary to establish or check the claim's truthfulness.

Key points and domain-specific details to guide your decomposition:

1. **Multi-hop claims**: These usually combine several entities, relationships, or attributes from different knowledge domains (e.g., geography, biographies, media, awards, filmographies, products, historical events). Your sub-questions should isolate each hop/step so its truth can be verified with focused evidence.

2. **Distinct information pieces**: Each sub-question should ask about a separate fact that is essential to verify part of the claim. Avoid merging multiple facts into one question. If the claim has multiple independent components (e.g., conjunctions or comparisons), formulate independent questions for each.

3. **Avoid ambiguity and clarify references**: 
   - Identify ambiguous or vague terms in the claim (such as names shared by multiple entities, pronouns, or imprecise descriptors).
   - Formulate sub-questions that clarify these ambiguities (e.g., specify the identity, location, or time period of an entity).
   - Ask for particulars that disambiguate (e.g., “Who is the co-writer of Film X with Person Y?” instead of just “Who co-wrote Film X?”).
   - When a claim compares or links two entities or groups, make sure sub-questions isolate the necessary details about each side independently.

4. **Target verifiable, factual information**: The sub-questions should ideally map directly to well-documented sources or data points (e.g., official bios, award records, production credits, geographic coordinates, event dates) that can validate each step in the claim’s reasoning.

5. **Structure for reasoning clarity**:

   - If the claim contains conjunctions or contrasting parts, create separate questions for each conjunct or claim half.
   - When dealing with relational or possessive claims (e.g., “brother of X,” “neighbor of Y”), one question should establish the relationship or identity, and another to verify the linked fact about that person/place/entity.
   - For claims involving rankings, operational statuses, or popularity measures, formulate questions extracting the relevant comparative metrics or timelines.
   - When the claim’s subject is a complex or compound phrase (such as “the actor who co-starred with X in series Y”), break this down by first identifying the entity, then verifying the attributed role or fact.

6. **Use precise, neutral wording** in the sub-questions that directly reflect the factual pieces needed from the claim, without assumptions or loaded language.

7. **Examples and domain-specific references encountered in prior data include, but are not limited to**:
   - Geographic locations and administrative entities (e.g., town served by a railway station, airport codes and operational history).
   - Media productions and filmographies (e.g., movie release years, cast members, roles, director credits).
   - Biographical and relationship details (e.g., family relations, award recipients, honorary degrees).
   - Product or game designers and comparative popularity.
   - Timelines of events and operational statuses.
   - Specificity for ambiguous names or titles (e.g., multiple films named “Zeitgeist,” several Leo Colovini games).
   - Popularity or reception metrics involving audience demographics or cultural reach.
   - Clarifying ambiguous quantifiers or measurements (e.g., “just under four miles” distance).

Overall, your goal is to produce clear, focused, independently verifiable sub-questions that collectively enable stepwise evaluation of every factual link in the original claim. This modularized approach helps identify which parts of the claim hold or fail, and supports transparent, fact-based verification.

Iteration 61: Proposed new text for gap_analyzer.predict: Your task is to critically analyze a complex, multi-entity factual verification claim given a structured input comprising:

1. **Claim**: A natural language statement involving multiple entities, facts, or relationships to be verified.

2. **Entities Found**: A list of extracted named entities and key concepts (persons, places, films, organizations, dates, events, products, etc.) mentioned in retrieved documents related to the claim.

3. **Relationships Found**: Explicit relationships, roles, or fact statements linking those entities as they appear in retrieved evidence.

4. **Documents Retrieved**: A count or summary of documents obtained thus far. 

Use these inputs to:

- Analyze the current evidence and reasoning achieved so far, identifying which parts of the claim are supported, contradicted, or remain ambiguous/unverified based on the extracted data.

- Identify precisely what critical information is still missing to conclusively verify (or refute) the claim. Missing information typically includes:  
  - Authoritative and primary source confirmations (e.g., official cast or production credits, biographical dates, place names, official organizational roles and titles, geographic data, taxonomy attributions, or comparable domain-specific authoritative records).  
  - Clear disambiguation between similarly named or ambiguous entities.  
  - Explicit documentary links establishing key relationships mentioned or required by the claim.  
  - Temporal or geographic clarifications needed for comparative or chronological claims.  
  - Clarifications of entity identities or roles where ambiguity exists (e.g., confirming which of several persons with the same name is referenced, or distinguishing a band's origin from an album's title).

- Identify specific targeted retrieval actions, expressed as detailed, focused search queries or database lookups, meant to obtain the missing information in the next research iteration. Queries should combine critical keywords, entity names, roles, dates, or locations, and specify suggested sources (e.g., site:imdb.com, official organizational pages, bio databases, or trusted news or encyclopedic sources).

Key domain-specific considerations to incorporate include:  

- **Film and media claims**: Confirm accurate film titles, release years, production and distribution companies, director and cast credits, filming locations, and relationship between works (sequels, prequels, remakes).  
- **Person biographies**: Verify birthdates, birthplaces, aliases, roles/professions, historical periods, and relationships to other entities (co-writers, band members, founders).  
- **Music and bands**: Differentiate between bands, albums, and songs; confirm roles of individual musicians; verify thematic genres or song classifications.  
- **Geographic comparisons**: Validate place names, city/region/country affiliations, temporal changes to names, and coordinate-based comparisons.  
- **Scientific/taxonomic claims**: Confirm formal taxonomy, naming attributions, native ranges, and whether species have the claimed characteristics.  
- **Organizational structures**: Identify exact organizational names, dates of founding, leadership roles, and affiliations relevant to the claim.

Use structured, clear, and domain-aware reasoning to lay out the current verification status. Then enumerate precise missing facts required to close verification gaps, framed as explicit information needs or questions. Finally, propose concise, concrete search queries or sources to target these information needs in the next data retrieval step.

Ensure to:  
- Disambiguate conflated or ambiguous entities and claims.  
- Separate primary fact confirmations from secondary or interpretive ones.  
- Distinguish between partial evidence (e.g., some data hinting but not confirming a fact) and complete, authoritative verification.  
- Reflect on the logical relationships in the claim, attending carefully to comparative or temporal phrasing.  
- Consider negative evidence or absence of data when relevant.  
- Avoid assuming facts absent from authoritative sources.

This approach facilitates iterative retrieval and refinement to substantiate complex knowledge claims through systematic evidence accumulation and clarification.
